Phylogenetic analysis of <i>SVPs</i> revealed that these two genes cluster close to <i>PtSVL</i> (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#MOESM2">2</a>: Fig. S16b), the function of which had been characterized as a repressor in the genetic network of temperature-mediated vegetative bud break in hybrid aspen [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Singh RK, Maurya JP, Azeez A, Miskolczi P, Tylewicz S, Stojkovič K, et al. A genetic network mediating the control of bud break in hybrid aspen. Nat Commun. 2018;9:4173." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d25660e1712">39</a>]. The downstream genes of <i>PtSVL</i> in the network including <i>TEOSINTE BRANCHED1</i>, <i>CYCLOIDEA</i>, <i>PCF/BRANCHED1</i> (<i>TCP18/BRC1</i>), and <i>FLOWERING LOCUS T</i> (<i>FT</i>), which function as negative and positive regulators of temperature-mediated control bud break, were also identified in the wintersweet genome (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#MOESM2">2</a>: Fig. S16c). During the transition from endodormancy to flush stage, the increase in expression of <i>CpFT1</i> and downregulation of <i>CpTCP18/BRC1</i> and <i>CpSVL1</i> were noted (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#MOESM2">2</a>: Fig. S16d). Gibberellin acid (GA) and abscisic acid (ABA) acts as positive and negative regulators of bud break respectively [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Liu JY, Sherif SM. Hormonal orchestration of bud dormancy cycle in deciduous woody perennials. Front Plant Sci. 2019;10:1136." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d25660e1753">40</a>], and the content of which to some extent is associated with the expression level of biosynthesis and catabolism genes. The increased expression of GA biosynthesis genes such as <i>GA20 oxidase</i> and the decreased expression of ABA biosynthesis genes such as the <i>NCED</i> (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#MOESM2">2</a>: Fig. S16d) was also observed at the bud break stage, which may coincide with their role in bud break.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Genetic basis of strong cold resistance</h3><p>Wintersweet is one of the perennial trees that bloom in the deep winter, during which the temperature always falls below the freezing point. Therefore, wintersweet has evolved a systematic mechanism to withstand cold stress. Volatile glycosylation is a common form of plant volatile compounds and plays an important role in response to abiotic stress in plants [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Song CK, Graphery H, Hoffmann T, Schwab W. Attractive but toxic: emerging roles of glycosidically bound volatiles and glycosyltransferases involved in their formation. Mol Plant. 2018;11:1225–36." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d25660e1774">41</a>]. Recent studies revealed that the volatile terpene glucosylation mediated by UDP-glycosyltransferases (UGTs) was involved in the modulation of cold stress tolerance in tea plants [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Zhao MY, Zhang N, Gao T, Jin JY, Jing TT, Wang JM, et al. Sesquiterpene glucosylation mediated by glucosyltransferase UGT91Q2 is involved in the modulation of cold stress tolerance in tea plants. New Phytol. 2019;226:362–72." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d25660e1777">42</a>]. In wintersweet, abundant volatiles were present in glycosidically bound forms, such as linalool glucoside, benzaldehyde benzyl alcohol (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#MOESM2">2</a>: Fig. S17). The considerable expansion in the UGT family (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S10) and abundant terpene glycosides in wintersweet flowers lead to the hypothesis that the strong cold tolerance of wintersweet, to some extent, is related to the volatile glucosylation.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Evolution of terpene biosynthesis and regulation-related genes</h3><p>Monoterpenes are the major components of floral volatile organic compounds (VOCs) in wintersweet, especially the linalool, which accounts for more than half of the floral scent [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Tian JP, Ma ZY, Zhao KG, Zhang J, Lin X, Chen LQ. Transcriptomic and proteomic approaches to explore the differences in monoterpene and benzenoid biosynthesis between scented and unscented genotypes of wintersweet. Physiol Plant. 2019;166:478–93." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d25660e1794">5</a>]. In plants, monoterpenes/diterpenes and sesquiterpenes are usually generated via the 2-<i>C</i>-methyl-D-erythritol 4-phosphate (MEP) pathway and the mevalonate (MVA) pathway, respectively [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Vranová E, Coman D, Gruissem W. Network analysis of the MVA and MEP pathways for isoprenoid synthesis. Annu Rev Plant Biol. 2013;64:665–700." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d25660e1800">43</a>]. A total of 46 genes in these two pathways were identified (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S18). The key genes involved in the MEP pathway such as 1-deoxy-<span class="u-small-caps">d</span>-xylulose 5-phosphate synthase (<i>DXS</i>), 1-deoxy-<span class="u-small-caps">d</span>-xylulose 5-phosphate reductoisomerase (<i>DXR</i>), and isopentenyl diphosphate isomerase (<i>IDI</i>) were generated through WGD events (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#Fig5">5</a>a). The high rate of paralog generation in these genes could increase the efficiency of catalytic reaction through dosage effects, thereby increasing the metabolic flux toward the MEP pathway. Terpene synthases (TPSs) are the enzymes responsible for the last catalytic reaction in the MVA and MEP pathway to generate terpenoid compounds. With the aid of the assembly genome, a total of 52 complete <i>CpTPSs</i> were identified (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S19), the number of which is approximately double that detected by transcriptomics in our previous study [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Tian JP, Ma ZY, Zhao KG, Zhang J, Lin X, Chen LQ. Transcriptomic and proteomic approaches to explore the differences in monoterpene and benzenoid biosynthesis between scented and unscented genotypes of wintersweet. Physiol Plant. 2019;166:478–93." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d25660e1832">5</a>]. Phylogenetic analysis of TPS from four species revealed that <i>CpTPSs</i> were clustered into five of six subfamilies described for land plants (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#Fig5">5</a>b). The majority of <i>CpTPSs</i> were placed in the TPS-a (18) and TPS-b (24) subfamilies, which is predominantly composed of angiosperm-specific sesquiterpene and monoterpene synthases respectively [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Chen F, Tholl D, Bohlmann J, Pichersky E. The family of terpene synthases in plants: a mid-size family of genes for specialized metabolism that is highly diversified throughout the kingdom. Plant J. 2011;66:212–29." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d25660e1844">44</a>]. Comparative genomics analysis revealed that the TPS genes are significantly expanded, especially in the TPS-b subfamily (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#Fig5">5</a>b and Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S10). These lineage-specific gene expansions in the TPS-b subfamily may contribute to the monoterpene accumulation in floral VOCs in wintersweet.
</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Fig. 5"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-020-02088-y/MediaObjects/13059_2020_2088_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-020-02088-y/MediaObjects/13059_2020_2088_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="641" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>Terpenes biosynthesis in wintersweet. <b>a</b> Expression profiles of genes encoding enzymes possibly involved in monoterpene and sesquiterpene biosynthesis. Abbreviations for enzymes in each catalytic step are shown in bold. The gradient color for each gene represents the gene expression levels in three floral developmental stages (S1: bud stage; S4: full open flower stage; S5: senescence stage). Homologous genes are represented by equal colored horizontal stripes and are termed from top to bottom in Arabic numerical order. The full names of enzymes are listed in the Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S18. The genes circled in green box were generated by WGD event. The non-boxed genes did not undergo this event. <b>b</b> Phylogeny of TPS proteins identified in wintersweet and 5 other sequenced plant genomes showing the subfamilies from a–g. <b>c</b> The expression of <i>CpTPS</i> genes in three different floral development stages. These genes were expressed in at least one of the three developmental stages. <b>d</b> The chromosomes with more than one <i>CpTPS</i> gene. The red diamonds represent the functionally characterized genes. The green diamonds represent the genes generated by tandem duplication events. The cure with arrow linked two duplicated genes. <b>e</b> Identification of enzymatic products after incubating recombinant CpTPSs proteins with geranyl diphosphate (GPP)/farnesyl diphosphate (FPP). The volatile terpenes were analyzed by GC-MS analysis and comparing with authentic standards. <b>f</b> Increased linalool biosynthesis in the <i>CpTPS4</i>-overexpressed <i>tobacco</i> compared with wild type (WT) and empty vector control (EV). Data represent the mean ± SDs of three biological replicates</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Expression analysis of the 52 <i>CpTPS</i> genes by RNA-seq revealed that six genes displayed similar expression patterns with the emission of major monoterpenes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#Fig5">5</a>c and Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#MOESM2">2</a>: Fig. S18). Based on the expression pattern and phylogenetic analysis, we further selected three genes from TPS-b/g subfamily for functional characterization and found that all the genes encoded versatile enzymes with multiple products (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#Fig5">5</a>e). Subcellular localization analysis showed that <i>CpTPS4</i> and <i>CpTPS9</i> were localized to the plastid whereas <i>CpTPS42</i> was targeted to the cytosol (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#MOESM2">2</a>: Fig. S19). <i>CpTPS42</i> was shown to be a sesquiterpene synthase, which mainly catalyzed the formation of nerolidol, together with other sesquiterpenes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#Fig5">5</a>e). <i>CpTPS4</i> and <i>CpTPS9</i> are both monoterpene synthases and produce β-pinene and linalool as its main product respectively (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#Fig5">5</a>e). To further understand the function of <i>CpTPS4</i>, we also overexpressed the gene in tobacco. Enhanced levels of the monoterpenes including linalool, limonene, β-ocimene, and trans-β-ocimene were found in transgenic tobacco leaves in comparison with the wild type control (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#Fig5">5</a>f). These results indicated that <i>CpTPS4</i> plays a primary role in the biosynthesis of linalool, the main components of floral scent.</p><p>The available genome assembly allows for <i>CpTPSs</i> to be localized to either chromosomes or scaffold positions to consider a genomic context. The <i>CpTPS</i> genes are not uniformly distributed throughout the chromosomes with 44 genes located on six chromosomes and eight genes on seven scaffolds (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#Fig5">5</a>d). Fourteen of the 52 <i>CpTPS</i> genes have at least two copies and each duplicated gene copy was located adjacent to the other. For example, <i>CpTPS4</i> is located on scaffold662 and has three copies including <i>CpTPS17</i>, <i>CpTPS18</i>, and <i>CpTPS19</i>. These three genes were arranged as a tandem array on chromosome 6 and highly expressed at the full open flower stage, which may have a similar function as <i>CpTPS4</i> and contribute equally to linalool production.</p><p>Terpenoid formation does not only depend on the biochemical properties of enzymes encoded by <i>CpTPS</i> genes but also requires the involvement of transcription factors (TFs). A total of 1313 TFs that show differential expression during petal development have been identified. Among these, 99 display positive correlation with the emission of terpenes and are predominantly distributed in MYB, bHLH, WRKY, and bZIP families (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#MOESM2">2</a>: Fig. S20 and Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S20). The transcriptional control of terpene biosynthesis genes correlates with the presence of cis-elements in their promoter regions, which were recognized and bound by specific transcription factors. When screening the 2000-bp regions upstream of the 52 <i>CpTPS</i> genes, several defense and stress responsive elements were found to be significantly enriched, such as bHLH- and MYB-binding elements (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#MOESM2">2</a>: Fig. S21). The results indicated that the MYB/bHLH transcription factors may serve as key factors in regulating the <i>CpTPS</i> genes expression and provide us with the starting point for the further studies to reveal the cross-talk in the regulation of plant secondary metabolites and stress responses.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Evolution of benzenoid/phenylpropanoid biosynthesis-related genes</h3><p>Benzenoids/phenylpropanoids are the second largest group of the floral VOCs in wintersweet, which are derived from the aromatic acid phenylalanine. Phenylalanine is synthesized via two pathways (phenylalanine pathway and aragenate pathway) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Maeda H, Dudareva N. The shikimate pathway and aromatic amino acid biosynthesis in plants. Annu Rev Plant Biol. 2012;63:73–105." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2020">45</a>], and these two pathways split from the plastidial shikimate pathway [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Qian YC, Lynch JH, Guo LY, Rhodes D, Morgan JA, Dudareva N. Completion of the cytosolic post-chorismate phenylalanine biosynthetic pathway in plants. Nat Commun. 2019;10:15." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2023">46</a>]. The genes involved in the shikimate pathway (20), phenylpyruvate pathway (7), and arogenate pathway (6) were identified as shown in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#Fig6">6</a>a. In the wintersweet genome, both WGD and tandem duplication events have considerably impacted both the upstream genes in the phenylpropanoid pathway and downstream genes involved in specific benzenoid (benzyl acetate and methyl salicylate) biosynthesis (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#Fig6">6</a>a,b), which lead to the high rate of paralog formation in 15 gene families (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S21).
</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Fig. 6"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 6</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-020-02088-y/MediaObjects/13059_2020_2088_Fig6_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-020-02088-y/MediaObjects/13059_2020_2088_Fig6_HTML.png" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1192" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p>Evolution and expression of key genes involved in benzenoid/phenylpropanoid biosynthesis. <b>a</b> Expression profiles of genes encoding enzymes possibly involved in the shikimate/benzenoid pathway in wintersweet. Abbreviations for enzymes in each catalytic step are shown in bold. The gradient color for each gene represents the gene expression levels in three petal developmental stages in wintersweet (S1: bud stage; S4: full open flower stage; S5: senescence stage). Homologous genes are represented by equal colored horizontal stripes and are termed from top to bottom in Arabic numerical order. The full names of enzymes are listed in the Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S21. The genes circled in black and red boxes were generated by WGD and tandem duplication events respectively. The non-boxed genes did not undergo these events. <b>b</b> Schematic representation of the wintersweet chromosomes together with the positions of key genes involved in benzenoid/phenylpropanoid biosynthesis. The genes marked in brown and red were generated by WGD and tandem duplication events respectively. <b>c</b> Expression profiles of the 21 <i>BEAT</i> homologous genes in three different stages (S1, S4, and S5). These genes were expressed in at least one of the three developmental stages</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Benzyl acetate, the dominant compounds of floral scent in wintersweet, is synthesized from benzyl alcohol via acetyl-CoA-dependent reaction catalyzed by acetyl-CoA: benzyl alcohol acetyltransferase (BEAT) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Aranovich D, Lewinsohn E, Zaccai M. Post-harvest enhancement of aroma in transgenic lisianthus (Eustoma grandiflorum) using the Clarkia breweri benzyl alcohol acetyltransferase (BEAT) gene. Postharvest Biol Technol. 2007;43:255–60." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2072">47</a>]. Comparative genomic analysis revealed that the wintersweet genome harbors 33 <i>BEAT</i> homologous genes (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S21), the number of which is comparable to that in <i>Prunus mume</i>, in which benzyl acetate is also the major component of floral scent. Similar to <i>P. mume</i>, the expansion of the <i>BEAT</i> homologous genes was mainly attributed to tandem and WGD duplication events [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Aranovich D, Lewinsohn E, Zaccai M. Post-harvest enhancement of aroma in transgenic lisianthus (Eustoma grandiflorum) using the Clarkia breweri benzyl alcohol acetyltransferase (BEAT) gene. Postharvest Biol Technol. 2007;43:255–60." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2091">47</a>]. Of 33 <i>BEAT</i> homologous genes found in wintersweet genome, 8 were derived from the WGD event, and 14 were amplified via tandem duplication. Transcriptome and metabolite correlation analysis showed that the expression pattern of 4 <i>CpBEATs</i> coincided with benzyl acetate emission (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#Fig6">6</a>c and Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#MOESM2">2</a>: Fig. S18). These genes might be responsible for benzyl acetate biosynthesis in the wintersweet flower. Methyl salicylate is also the major composition of floral VOCs in wintersweet. Three tandem duplication-derived salicylic acid methyltransferase (<i>SAMTs</i>) were identified in the wintersweet (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#MOESM2">2</a>: Fig. S22), two of which were highly expressed in the flower and their expression patterns correlated with methyl salicylate emission, suggesting that these two genes may be primarily responsible for methyl salicylate biosynthesis (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#Fig6">6</a>a). These observations above suggested that the expansion of specific genes and selective expression in flower could induce the heightened activity of the corresponding enzymes, which resulted in the abundant characteristic aroma formation in the flowers of wintersweet.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec12" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec12-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec12">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec12-content"><p>In this study, we constructed a high-quality chromosome-level genome assembly for wintersweet by combining the long-read sequences from PacBio with highly accurate short reads from Illumina sequencing and using Hi-C data for super-scaffolding. The assembly of wintersweet adds to the growing body of genome information for the Calycantaceae family. As the relatively domesticated species in the Calycantaceae family [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Zhang R, Shen X. Taxonomy geographical distribution and evolution of Calycanthaceae. J Beijing Forestry University. 1999;21:7–11." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2125">48</a>], wintersweet has a range of specific biological features such as early blooming in deep winter, strong cold resistance, and fragrant flowers [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Zhao Y, Zhang Y, Wang ZZ. Chemical composition and biological activities of essential oil from flower of Chimonanthus praecox (L.) link. Lishizhen Med Material Med Res. 2010;21:622–5." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2128">4</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Xiang L, Zhao KG, Chen LQ. Molecular cloning and expression of Chimonanthus praecox farnesyl pyrophosphate synthase gene and its possible involvement in the biosynthesis of floral volatile sesquiterpenoids. Plant Physiol Biochem. 2010;48:845–50." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2131">10</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Sui SZ, Luo JH, Ma J, Zhu Q, Lei XH. Generation and analysis of expressed sequence tags from Chimonanthus praecox (Wintersweet) flowers for discovering stress-responsive and floral development-related genes. Comparative Functional Genomics. 2012;2012:134596." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2134">49</a>]. As a representative of the Magnoliids, it also maintains a key evolutionary position on the tree of life. The availability of the wintersweet genome sequence makes it possible to consider deep angiosperm phylogenic questions, determine genome evolution signatures, and to reveal the genetic basis of interesting traits. This assembly also facilitates in-depth fundamental comparative genomic analysis to elucidate biology and gain resolution of genome evolution between wintersweet and other species within the Calycantaceae family.</p><p>Resolution of the relationship among Magnoliids, monocots, and eudicots has not been conclusively determined, despite numerous attempts. In four independent studies, four genomes representing three orders (Magnoliales, Piperales, and Laurales) within magnoliales have been published [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chaw SM, Liu YC, Wu YW, Wang HY, Ling CY, Wu CS, et al. Stout camphor tree genome fills gaps in understanding of flowering plant genome evolution. Nat Plants. 2019;5:63." href="#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2140">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chen JH, Hao ZD, Guang XM, Zhao CX, Wang PK, Xue LJ, et al. Liriodendron genome sheds light on angiosperm phylogeny and species–pair differentiation. Nat Plants. 2019;5:18." href="#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2140_1">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Rendón-Anaya M, Ibarra-Laclette E, Bravo AM, Lan TY, Zheng CF, Carretero-Paulet L, et al. The avocado genome informs deep angiosperm phylogeny, highlights introgressive hybridization, and reveals pathogen influenced gene space adaptation. Proc Natl Acad Sci. 2019;116:17081–9." href="#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2140_2">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Hu LS, Xu ZP, Wang MJ, Fan R, Yuan DJ, Wu BD, et al. The chromosome-scale reference genome of black pepper provides insight into piperine biosynthesis. Nat Commun. 2019;10:1–11." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2143">16</a>], and each study attempted to resolve the phylogenetic position of magnoliidaes. Three species including <i>Piper nigrum</i> (representative of Piperales clade), <i>L. tulipifera</i> (representative of Magnoliales clade), and <i>P. americana</i> (representative of laurales clade) were placed as sister to the monocots and eudicots, while <i>C. kanehirae</i> (representative of Magnoliales clade) was found to be a sister clade to the eudicots. Many factors could be responsible for these topological differences such as taxon sample size [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Leebens-Mack J, Raubeson LA, Cui L, Kuehl JV, Fourcade MH, Fourcade TW, et al. Identifying the basal angiosperm node in chloroplast genome phylogenies: sampling one's way out of the Felsenstein zone. Mol Biol Evol. 2005;22:1948–63." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2159">50</a>], possible incomplete lineage sorting (ILS) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Yang YZ, Sun PC, Lv L, Wang DL, Ru DF, Li Y, et al. Prickly waterlily and rigid hornwort genomes shed light on early angiosperm evolution. Nat Plants. 2020;6:215–22." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2162">17</a>], and the number of retrieved orthologs [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Zeng L, Zhang Q, Sun RR, Kong HZ, Zhang N, Ma H. Resolution of deep angiosperm phylogeny using conserved nuclear genes and estimates of early divergence times. Nat Commun. 2014;5:4956." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2165">51</a>]. For example, adequate taxon sampling, especially those smaller sister lineages such as Chloranthales in angiosperm clades [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Soltis DE, Soltis PS. Amborella not a “basal angiosperm”? Not so fast. Am J Bot. 2004;91:997–1001." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2168">52</a>], was vital to obtain a resolved phylogeny. To account for incomplete lineage sorting, we used two complementary tools to extract the single-copy genes and two methods (coalescent and concatenation-based analysis) to reconstruct the phylogeny. In addition, we also improved taxon sampling, selected key lineages (representative of chloranthales clade) as well as additional lineages in the monocots and eudicots, and included five magnoliids to cover key representative clades. Finally, all the analyses recovered the magnoliids together with eudicots as sister to the monocots. This result is congruent with a recent study of 59 low-copy nuclear genes from 26 mesangiosperm transcriptomes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Zeng L, Zhang Q, Sun RR, Kong HZ, Zhang N, Ma H. Resolution of deep angiosperm phylogeny using conserved nuclear genes and estimates of early divergence times. Nat Commun. 2014;5:4956." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2171">51</a>] and 410 single-copy genes nuclear gene families extracted from genomic and transcriptomic data from 1153 species [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Leebens-Mack JH, Barker MS, Carpenter EJ, Deyholos MK, Gitzendanner MA. One thousand plant transcriptomes and the phylogenomics of green plants. Nature. 2019;574:679." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2175">53</a>], but disagrees with the plastid trees which supported a topology of magnoliales as the sister to monocots and eudicots. In comparison with nuclear genes, the plastid genes are uniparentally inherited and may recover different deep-level relationships resulting from ancient lineage sorting and hybridization, which might potentially introduce biases and errors to phylogenetic reconstruction [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Zeng L, Zhang Q, Sun RR, Kong HZ, Zhang N, Ma H. Resolution of deep angiosperm phylogeny using conserved nuclear genes and estimates of early divergence times. Nat Commun. 2014;5:4956." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2178">51</a>]. To date, the genome data were still absent in the key clades of angiosperms, such as Chloranthales. Even though we have suggested a robust phylogeny using “genome-scale” data, sequencing of the complete angiosperm lineages will facilitate future investigations of the phylogenetic relationships of flowering plants.</p><p>Wintersweet is one of the very few flowering plant lineages that bloom in winter, which make it an ideal perennial plant for flowering-time study. Application of database about the flowering-time gene networks in <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> serves to identify the homologs of flowering-time genes in wintersweet. Comparative transcriptome analyses provide an array of resources for further flowering-time-related gene identification. Mapping quantitative trait loci (QTL) onto linkage maps, with segregating genetic populations, is a powerful strategy to dissecting complex agronomical characters [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Xie DS, Xu YC, Wang JP, Liu WR, Zhou Q, Luo SB, et al. The wax gourd genomes offer insights into the genetic diversity and ancestral cucurbit karyotype. Nat Commun. 2019;10:5158." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2187">54</a>]. The availability of high-quality genome and diverse germplasm of wintersweet with different flowering time makes it possible to use this genetic approach to detect flowering-time quantitative trait loci in the future. The petaloid sepal is another striking distinction of wintersweet. This flower structure also exists in some basal eudicots (such as <i>Ranunculus</i> and <i>Aquilegia</i>), some monocots (such as <i>Liliumus</i> and <i>Tulipa</i>), and basal angiosperm lineages, which was supposed to be displayed by the ancestral angiosperm flower [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Sauquet H, Balthazar MV, Magallón S, Doyle JA, Endress PK, Bailes EJ, et al. The ancestral flower of angiosperms and its early diversification. Nat Commun. 2017;8:16047." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2203">55</a>]. The broad expression pattern of B-function genes was shared by these species, which may represent the ancestral condition for angiosperms. The genetic network for seasonal temperature-mediated control of bud break has been elucidated in the vegetable bud of hybrid aspen [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Singh RK, Maurya JP, Azeez A, Miskolczi P, Tylewicz S, Stojkovič K, et al. A genetic network mediating the control of bud break in hybrid aspen. Nat Commun. 2018;9:4173." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2206">39</a>]. In this genetic network, the <i>FT</i> and <i>SVL</i> are the homologs of <i>FT</i> and <i>SVP</i> in Arabidopsis respectively, both of which act as a flowering regulator [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Lee JH, Yoo SJ, Park SH, Hwang I, Lee JS, Ahn JH. Role of SVP in the control of flowering time by ambient temperature in Arabidopsis. Genes Dev. 2007;21:397–402." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2222">56</a>]. Similar to the vegetable buds, the floral buds are also subjected to the dormancy and bud break. The homologs of the key components in wintersweet displayed a similar expression pattern during the transition from endodormancy to bud break stage, leading us to the hypothesis that wintersweet may utilize the common signaling components in both flowering and bud break process.</p><p>The evolution, adaptation, and domestication of wintersweet resulted in specific qualities and quantities of floral volatiles, primarily consisting of monoterpenes and benzenoids [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Deng C, Song G, Hu Y. Rapid determination of volatile compounds emitted from Chimonanthus praecox flowers by HS-SPME-GC-MS. Z Naturforsch C. 2004;59:636–40." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2228">7</a>]. The diversification of terpenes is mainly determined by the <i>TPS</i> family genes, among which the <i>TPS</i>-b subfamily is well known for monoterpenes synthesis [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Chen F, Tholl D, Bohlmann J, Pichersky E. The family of terpene synthases in plants: a mid-size family of genes for specialized metabolism that is highly diversified throughout the kingdom. Plant J. 2011;66:212–29." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2237">44</a>]. The extensive expansion of <i>TPS</i>-b subfamily genes in the wintersweet genome may be one explanation for diverse monoterpene accumulation. The production of terpenes is regulated to a large extent by the transcription level of <i>TPS</i> genes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Vranová E, Coman D, Gruissem W. Network analysis of the MVA and MEP pathways for isoprenoid synthesis. Annu Rev Plant Biol. 2013;64:665–700." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2247">43</a>]. The results of the present expression analyses revealed a dynamic expression of the <i>TPS</i> genes, which may be another explanation for the monoterpene diversification. Using the genomic data, we found remarkable duplications of the metabolic genes in both terpene and benzoid/phenylpropanoid biosynthesis pathways, especially in the <i>TPS</i> and <i>BEAT</i> genes which are responsible for the major components (linalool and benzyl acetate) production. Tandem duplication is the major contributor to the expansions of <i>TPS</i> and <i>BEAT</i> genes and most of these duplicated genes are tandemly organized in clusters. In the <i>Drosophila melanogaster</i> genome, the <i>Adh</i> gene is tandemly duplicated and shows a 2.6-fold greater expression than the single-copy gene. The overactivity caused by the tandem arrangement was proposed to be a general property of tandem gene duplicates [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Salamov AA, Solovyev VV. Ab initio gene finding in Drosophila genomic DNA. Genome Res. 2000;10:516–22." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2272">57</a>]. The greater output of the tandem arrangement in the <i>TPS</i> and <i>BEAT</i> genes may increase transcript abundance of the tandem duplicates and thereby led to the mass production of major components. Based on our data, we speculate that the remarkable duplication, tandem clustering of gene, and gene expression dynamics may contribute to the abundant characteristic aroma formation in wintersweet.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec13" data-title="Conclusions"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec13-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec13">Conclusions</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec13-content"><p>In summary, we have presented a high-quality assembly of wintersweet, the first genome reference in the Calycantaceae family. The integration of multi-omics data advanced our understanding of floral scent biochemistry as well as the molecular mechanisms underlying flowering. Over a thousand years of wintersweet cultivation, evolutionary forces have shaped abundant cultivars with novelty in floral scent and color. The available complete wintersweet genome will provide a fundamental resource for comparative genomics studies on the diversity and the evolutionary mechanisms of ornamental traits (floral scent and color) at the genome level, which will be invaluable for genetic improvement through molecular breeding in the future.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec14" data-title="Materials and methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec14-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec14">Materials and methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec14-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Plant materials, library construction, and genome sequencing</h3><p>All plant materials used in this study were collected from the campus of Huazhong Agriculture University (Wuhan, China) under natural photoperiod. High-quality, intact genomic DNA was extracted from fresh leaves of <i>Chimonanthus praecox “H29”</i> and <i>Calycanthus chinensis</i> using DNAsecure Plant Kit (TIANGEN). The isolated DNA was quantified using a NanoDrop D-100 spectrometer (Nanodrop Technologies) according to the manufacturer’s protocol. A high-quality genome assembly for the <i>C. praecox</i> genome was achieved by using three sequencing methods that include Illumina paired-end and mate-pair sequencing, 10X Genomics sequencing, and single molecule real-time (SMRT) sequencing from Pacific Biosciences (PacBio).</p><p>A total of 10 μg of sheared DNA was used for a 20-kb insert size library subsequently sequenced on the Pacbio Sequel platform. A short-read genomic library was prepared using a Illumina TruSeq library construction kit according to the manufacturer’s instructions (Illumina, San Diego, CA). A total of 6 libraries with insert sizes ranging from 250 bp to 20 kb were constructed for <i>C</i>. <i>chinensis</i> and one library with a 350-bp insert size was constructed for <i>C. praecox</i>. The libraries were then sequenced on the Illumina HiSeqXTen platform. For the 10X Genomics library, about 1 ng input DNA with an average 50 kb length was used for the GEM reaction procedure during PCR, and 16-bp barcodes were introduced into droplets. The droplets were then fractured followed by final purification of the DNA library. A total of six libraries were finally sequenced on the Illumina HiseqXTen.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Genome assembly and assessment of the assembly quality</h3><p>De novo assembly of the PacBio reads was performed using the FALCON assembler [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Chin CS, Peluso P, Sedlazeck FJ, Nattestad M, Concepcion GT, Clum A, et al. Phased diploid genome assembly with single-molecule real-time sequencing. Nat Methods. 2016;13:1050." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2330">18</a>] (<a href="https://github.com/PacificBiosciences/FALCON/">https://github.com/PacificBiosciences/FALCON/</a>). Before assembly, we used FALCON to correct the PacBio reads and then assemble them into contigs with parameters (length_cutoff_pr = 4000, max_diff = 100, max_cov = 100). This resulted in primary contigs (p-contigs) that were then polished using Quiver [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Chin CS, Alexander DH, Marks P, Klammer AA, Drake J, Heiner C, et al. Nonhybrid, finished microbial genome assemblies from long-read SMRT sequencing data. Nat Methods. 2013;10:563." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2340">58</a>] by aligning SMRT reads. Pilon was used to perform the second round of error correction with short paired-end reads generated from Illumina HiSeq Platforms. For the scaffolding step, Long Ranger (version 2.1.2) (<a href="https://support.10xgenomics.com/genome-exome/software/pipelines/latest/installation">https://support.10xgenomics.com/genome-exome/software/pipelines/latest/installation</a>) was first used to build scaffolds using 10X data. FragScaff (version 1.1) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Adey A, Kitzman JO, Burton JN, Daza R, Kumar A, Christiansen L, et al. In vitro, long-range sequence information for de novo genome assembly via transposase contiguity. Genome Res. 2014;24:2041–9." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2350">59</a>] was further applied to build super-scaffolds using the barcoded sequencing reads.</p><p>We applied both CEGMA (Core Eukaryotic Gene Mapping Approach) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Parra G, Bradnam K, Korf I. CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes. Bioinformatics. 2007;23:1061–7." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2356">60</a>] and BUSCO (V3, Benchmarking Universal Single-Copy Orthologs) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Simão FA, Waterhouse RM, Ioannidis P, Kriventseva EV, Zdobnov EM. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics. 2015;31:3210–2." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2359">61</a>] to evaluate the completeness of the assembly.</p><p>For the assembly of <i>C. chinensis</i>, we first used SOAPdenovo [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Li RQ, Zhu HM, Ruan J, Qian WB, Fang XD, Shi ZB, et al. De novo assembly of human genomes with massively parallel short read sequencing. Genome Res. 2010;20:265–72." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2368">62</a>] to filter duplicates, adaptor contamination, and low-quality bases from Illumina PE reads before assembling them into preliminary scaffolds. Then GapCloser (version 1.12) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Luo R, Liu B, Xie Y, Li Z, Huang WH, Yuan JY. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience. 2012;1:18." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2371">63</a>] from the SOAPdenovo package was used for gap filling within assembled scaffolds using all pair-end reads. Lastly, SSPACE [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Boetzer M, Henkel CV, Jansen HJ, Butler D, Pirovano W. Scaffolding pre-assembled contigs using SSPACE. Bioinformatics. 2010;27:578–9." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2374">64</a>] was used to improve the assembled genome.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Chromosome assignment using Hi-C</h3><p>One Dovetail Hi-C library was prepared in a similar manner as described previously [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Lieberman-Aiden E, Van Berkum NL, Williams L, Imakaev M, Ragoczy T, Telling A, et al. Comprehensive mapping of long-range interactions reveals folding principles of the human genome. Science. 2009;326:289–93." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2385">65</a>]. The library was sequenced on an Illumina HiSeq platform. The number and length of read pairs produced for the library was 431 million and 2 × 100 bp respectively. The Dovetail Hi-C library reads provided 93.06 × physical coverage of the genome (1–50 kb pairs). The Hi-C sequencing data was aligned to the assembled contigs with BWA-mem [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Li H, Durbin R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics. 2009;25:1754–60." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2388">66</a>], and then clustered onto chromosomes with LACHESIS (<a href="http://shendurelab.github.io/LACHESIS/">http://shendurelab.github.io/LACHESIS/</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Repeat and non-coding RNA annotation</h3><p>We used two complementary methods (one homology-based and de novo-based) to discover and classify transposable elements (TEs). The homology-based repeat library was generated from a known repeat library (Repbase 15.02) using RepeatMasker (version 3.3.0) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Chen NS. Using Repeat Masker to identify repetitive elements in genomic sequences. Curr Protoc Bioinformatics. 2004;5:4–10." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2406">67</a>]. RepeatModeler (Vision 1.0.5) (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/">http://www.repeatmasker.org/</a>), RepeatScout [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Price AL, Jones NC, Pevzner PA. De novo identification of repeat families in large genomes. Bioinformatics. 2005;21:i351–8." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2416">68</a>], Piler, and LTR_FINDER [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Xu Z, Wang H. LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 2007;35:W265–8." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2419">69</a>] were used to build the de novo-based repeat library. RepeatProteinMask was performed to detect TEs in the <i>C. praecox</i> and <i>C. chinensis</i> genome by comparing the TE protein database. The integrated repeat library was finally annotated by the Tandem Repeats Finder (TRF) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Benson G. Tandem repeats finder: a program to analyze DNA sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 1999;27:573–80." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2429">70</a>]. The tRNA genes were predicted using tRNAscan-SE software [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Lowe TM, Eddy SR. tRNAscan-SE: a program for improved detection of transfer RNA genes in genomic sequence. Nucleic Acids Res. 1997;25:955–64." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2432">71</a>]. The rRNA, miRNA, and snRNA fragments were predicted using INFERNAL software [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Nawrocki EP, Kolbe DL, Eddy SR. Infernal 1.0: inference of RNA alignments. Bioinformatics. 2009;25:1335–7." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2435">72</a>] with searches against the Rfam database (release 9.1) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Griffiths-Jones S, Moxon S, Marshall M, Khanna A, Eddy SR, Bateman A. Rfam: annotating non-coding RNAs in complete genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 2005;33:D121–4." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2438">73</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Structural and functional annotation of genes</h3><p>We adapted a combination of three strategies that include (1) de novo predictions, (2) homolog-based predictions, and (3) RNA-seq-based predictions to annotate the protein-coding genes in the <i>C. praecox</i> and <i>C. chinensis</i> genomes. De novo predictions were carried out using five ab initio gene prediction programs that include Augustus (version 3.0.2) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Stanke M, Schoffmann O, Morgenstern, B, Waack S Gene prediction in eukaryotes with a generalized hidden Markov model that uses hints from external sources. BMC Bioinformatics 2006;7:62." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2456">74</a>], Genscan (version 1.0) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Li RQ, Zhu HM, Ruan J, Qian WB, Fang XD, Shi ZB, et al. De novo assembly of human genomes with massively parallel short read sequencing. Genome Res. 2010;20:265–72." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2459">62</a>], Geneid [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Parra G, Blanco E, Guigó R. Geneid in drosophila. Genome Res. 2000;10:511–5." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2462">75</a>], GlimmerHMM (version 3.0.2) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Majoros WH, Pertea M, Salzberg SL. TigrScan and GlimmerHMM: two open source ab initio eukaryotic gene-finders. Bioinformatics. 2004;20:2878–9." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2466">76</a>], and SNAP [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Korf I. Gene finding in novel genomes. BMC Bioinformatics. 2004;5:59." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2469">77</a>]. For the homolog-based predictions, the protein sequences of six species namely <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>Oryza sativa</i>, <i>Nelumbo nucifera</i>, <i>Beta vulgaris</i>, <i>Solanum lycopersicuma</i>, and <i>Vitis vinifera</i> were aligned against the repeat-masked genome using TBLASTN [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Altschul SF, Madden TL, Schäffer AA, Zhang JH, Zhang Z, Miller W, Lipman DJ. Gapped BLAST and PSI-BLAST: a new generation of protein database search programs. Nucleic Acids Res. 1997;25:3389–402." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2491">78</a>] with a cutoff <i>E</i>-value of 10<sup>− 5</sup>. Genewise (version 2.2.0) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Birney E, Clamp M, Durbin R. GeneWise and genomewise. Genome Res. 2004;14:988–95." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2499">79</a>] was employed to predict gene models based on the alignment sequences. We used two methods to achieve RNA-seq-based predictions. One was mapping the RNA-seq data to the <i>C. praecox</i> and <i>C</i>. <i>chinensis</i> genome and assembling the transcripts using Tophat (version 2.0.8) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 80" title="Trapnell C, Pachter L, Salzberg SL. TopHat: discovering splice junctions with RNA-Seq. Bioinformatics. 2009;25:1105–11." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR80" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2512">80</a>] and cufflinks (version 2.1.1) (<a href="http://cufflinks.cbcb.umd.edu/">http://cufflinks.cbcb.umd.edu/</a>) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 81" title="Trapnell C, Williams BA, Pertea G, Mortazavi A, Kwan G, Baren MJV, Salzberg SL, Wold BJ, Pachter L. Transcript assembly and quantification by RNA-Seq reveals unannotated transcripts and isoform switching during cell differentiation. Nat Biotechnol. 2010;28:511." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR81" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2522">81</a>]. The other was applying Trinity [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 82" title="Grabherr MG, Haas BJ, Yassour M, Levin JZ, Thompson DA, Amit I, et al. Full-length transcriptome assembly from RNA-Seq data without a reference genome. Nat Biotechnol. 2011;29:644." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR82" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2526">82</a>] to assemble the RNA-seq data followed by the PASA software (<a href="http://pasapipeline.github.io/">http://pasapipeline.github.io/</a>) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 83" title="Haas BJ, Delcher AL, Mount SM, Wortman JR, Jr RKS, Hannick LI, et al. Improving the Arabidopsis genome annotation using maximal transcript alignment assemblies. Nucleic Acids Res 2003;31:5654–5666." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR83" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2536">83</a>] to improve the gene structures. To finalize the gene set, all the predictions were combined using EVidenceModeler (EVM) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 84" title="Haas BJ, Salzberg SL, Zhu W, Pertea M, Allen JE, Orvis J, White O, Buell CR, Wortman JR. Automated eukaryotic gene structure annotation using EVidenceModeler and the program to assemble spliced alignments. Genome Biol. 2008;9:R7." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR84" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2539">84</a>] to produce the non-redundant gene sets.</p><p>Functional annotation of protein-coding genes was carried out by performing BLASTP (<i>E</i>-value ≤1e<sup>− 5</sup>) searches against SwissProt (<a href="http://www.uniprot.org/">http://www.uniprot.org/</a>), TrEMBL [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 85" title="Bairoch A, Apweiler R. The SWISS-PROT protein sequence database and its supplement TrEMBL in 2000. Nucleic Acids Res. 2000;28:45–8." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR85" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2557">85</a>], and NCBI non-redundant (NR) protein databases. Motifs and domains were annotated by using InterProScan (version 4.7) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 86" title="Zdobnov EM, Apweiler R. InterProScan–an integration platform for the signature-recognition methods in InterPro. Bioinformatics. 2001;17:847–8." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR86" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2560">86</a>] to search against InterPro (v29.0) databases which include Pfam, PRINTS, PROSITE, ProDom, and SMART. The GO term [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 87" title="Ashburner M, Ball CA, Blake JA, Botstein D, Butler H, Cheery JM, et al. Gene ontology: tool for the unification of biology. Nat Genet. 2000;25:25." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR87" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2564">87</a>] for each gene was achieved from the corresponding InterPro descriptions. Additionally, the gene set was mapped to the KEGG (release 53) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 88" title="Kanehisa M, Goto S. KEGG: Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 2000;28:27–30." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR88" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2567">88</a>] pathway database to identify the best match classification for each gene.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Gene family and phylogenomic analysis</h3><p>To identify gene family groups, we analyzed protein-coding genes from 17 species, <i>Amborella trichopoda</i>, <i>Ananas comosus</i>, <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>Camellia sinensis, C. kanehirae</i>, <i>Daucus carota</i>, <i>Elaeis guineensis</i>, <i>L. chinense</i>, <i>Musa acuminata</i>, <i>N. nucifera</i>, <i>O. sativa</i>, <i>Papaver somniferum</i>, <i>Phalaenopsis equestris</i>, <i>S. lycopersicum</i>, <i>V. vinifera</i>, <i>C. praecox</i>, and <i>C</i>. <i>chinensis</i> genomes. Orthologous gene groups of <i>C. praecox</i> and 16 other species were identified by running OrthoMCL program (<a href="http://orthomcl.org/orthomcl/">http://orthomcl.org/orthomcl/</a>) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Li L, Stoeckert CJ, Roos DS. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 2003;13:2178–89." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2642">23</a>]. We determined the expansion and contraction of the gene families by comparing the cluster size differences between the ancestor and each species using the CAFÉ program [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 89" title="Bie TD, Cristianini N, Demuth JP, Hahn MW. CAFE: a computational tool for the study of gene family evolution. Bioinformatics. 2006;22:1269–71." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR89" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2645">89</a>].</p><p>To infer the phylogenetic placements of wintersweet, two sets of single-copy genes (SSCG and OSCG) were identified using SonicParanoid [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Cosentino S, Iwasaki W. SonicParanoid: fast, accurate and easy orthology inference. Bioinformatics. 2019;35:149–51." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2651">24</a>] v.1.0 and OrthoMCL [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Li L, Stoeckert CJ, Roos DS. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 2003;13:2178–89." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2654">23</a>] v.2.0.9 from 17 seed plants respectively. For each gene, amino acid sequences were aligned using MUSCLE [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 90" title="Edgar RC. MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput. Nucleic Acids Res. 2004;32:1792–7." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR90" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2657">90</a>]. For each dataset SSCG and OSCG, the phylogenetic trees were constructed by both coalescent and concatenation approaches. For the coalescent approach, gene trees were inferred by IQ-TREE [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 91" title="Nguyen LT, Schmidt HA, Haeseler AV, Minh BQ. IQ-TREE: a fast and effective stochastic algorithm for estimating maximum-likelihood phylogenies. Mol Biol Evol. 2015;32:268–74." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR91" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2660">91</a>] v.1.6.9; these gene trees were then used by ASTRAL [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Massoni J, Couvreur TLP, Sauquet H. Five major shifts of diversification through the long evolutionary history of Magnoliidae (angiosperms). BMC Evol Biol. 2015;15:49." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2663">25</a>] v.5.6.1 to construct species trees. The quartet score was estimated for each node showing quartet support for the species tree. For the concatenation-based analyses, the ML trees were inferred from the concatenated amino acid sequences using IQ-TREE [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 91" title="Nguyen LT, Schmidt HA, Haeseler AV, Minh BQ. IQ-TREE: a fast and effective stochastic algorithm for estimating maximum-likelihood phylogenies. Mol Biol Evol. 2015;32:268–74." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR91" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2667">91</a>] v.1.6.9 with ultrafast bootstrap testing (1000 replicates) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 92" title="Hoang DT, Chernomor O, Haeseler AV, Minh BQ, Vinh LS. UFBoot2: improving the ultrafast bootstrap approximation. Mol Biol Evol. 2018;35:518–22." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR92" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2670">92</a>]. For plastid genes, the extrons of 38 single-copy genes were extracted from 26 plastid genome sequences. We aligned these genes with MUSCLE [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 90" title="Edgar RC. MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput. Nucleic Acids Res. 2004;32:1792–7." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR90" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2673">90</a>]. The concatenated amino acid sequences of these genes were used to infer the ML trees with RAxML [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 93" title="Stamatakis A. RAxML-VI-HPC: maximum likelihood-based phylogenetic analyses with thousands of taxa and mixed models. Bioinformatics. 2006;22:2688–290." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR93" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2676">93</a>]. The mcmctree program of PAML (<a href="http://abacus.gene.ucl.ac.uk/software/paml.html">http://abacus.gene.ucl.ac.uk/software/paml.html</a>) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 94" title="Yang Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol Biol Evol. 2007;24:1586–91." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR94" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2686">94</a>] was applied to estimate divergence time among 17 species with main parameters (burn-in = 10,000, sample-number = 100,000, and sample-frequency = 2). Four calibration points were selected from articles and TimeTree website (<a href="http://www.timetree.org">http://www.timetree.org</a>) as normal priors to restrain the age of the nodes, such as 105–115 Mya between <i>V. vinifera</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i>, 110–124 Mya between <i>C. sinensis</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i>, 148–173 Mya between <i>O. sativa</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i>, and 94–115 Mya between <i>A. comosus</i> and <i>O. sativa</i>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Analysis of genome synteny and whole-genome duplication</h3><p>We performed synteny searches to identify syntenic blocks within <i>C. praecox</i> and between <i>C. praecox</i> and <i>Amborella</i> using MCScanX [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 95" title="Wang YP, Tang HB, Debarry JD, Tan X, Li JP, Wang XY, et al. MCScanX: a toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and collinearity. Nucleic Acids Res. 2012;40:e49." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR95" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2739">95</a>]. Synonymous substitutions per synonymous site (<i>K</i>s) between colinear genes were estimated using the codeml approach as implemented in the PAML package [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 94" title="Yang Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol Biol Evol. 2007;24:1586–91." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR94" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2746">94</a>]. Calculation and correction of Ks of collinear blocks with reference to Prickly waterlily and rigid hornwort genome [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Yang YZ, Sun PC, Lv L, Wang DL, Ru DF, Li Y, et al. Prickly waterlily and rigid hornwort genomes shed light on early angiosperm evolution. Nat Plants. 2020;6:215–22." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2749">17</a>] and Paleotetraploidization study in Cucurbitaceae [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 96" title="Wang JP, Sun PC, Li YX, Liu YZ, Yang NS, Yu JG, et al. An overlooked Paleotetraploidization in Cucurbitaceae. Mol Biol Evol. 2018;35:16–26." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR96" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2752">96</a>] as follows: Firstly, the median Ks values were selected to represent each syntenic block, and the probability density distribution curve of Ks was estimated using MATLAB with the kernel smoothing density function. Multipeak fitting of the curve was performed using the Gaussian approximation function (cftool) in MATLAB. Secondly, based on Ks distribution of <i>Liriodendron</i> and <i>Cinnamomum</i> described in Prickly waterlily and rigid hornwort genome [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Yang YZ, Sun PC, Lv L, Wang DL, Ru DF, Li Y, et al. Prickly waterlily and rigid hornwort genomes shed light on early angiosperm evolution. Nat Plants. 2020;6:215–22." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2761">17</a>], the Ks correction coefficient of species of <i>Magnolia</i> were calculated and then we got the corrected Ks rate. The Dot plots between <i>C. praecox</i> and <i>C. kanehirae</i> as well as the <i>L. chinense</i> genome was generated with Quota synteny alignment software [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 97" title="Tang HB, Lyons E, Pedersen B, Schnable JC, Paterson AH, Freeling M. Screening synteny blocks in pairwise genome comparisons through integer programming. BMC Bioinformatics. 2011;12:102." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR97" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2777">97</a>] to visualize the paleopolyploidy level of <i>C. praecox</i> in relation to <i>C. kanehirae</i> and <i>L. chinense</i>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">Ancestral genome reconstruction</h3><p>The ancestral genome of Laurales was reconstructed according to the procedure (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#MOESM2">2</a>: Fig. S15) as described previously [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 98" title="Murat F, Zhang RZ, Guizard S, Gavranović H, Flores R, Steinbach D, Quesneville H, et al. Karyotype and gene order evolution from reconstructed extinct ancestors highlight contrasts in genome plasticity of modern Rosid crops. Genome Biol Evol. 2015;7:735–49." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR98" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2801">98</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 99" title="Murat F, Armero A, Pont C, Klopp C, Salse J. Reconstructing the genome of the most recent common ancestor of flowering plants. Nat Genet. 2017;49:490–6." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR99" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2804">99</a>]. The ancestral karyotype of <i>C. praecox</i>, <i>C. chinensis</i>, and <i>C. kanehirae</i> was first determined by genome alignments using cumulative identity percentage (CIP) and cumulative alignment length percentage (CALP) BLAST parameters [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 100" title="Salse J, Abrouk M, Murat F, Quraishi UM, Feuillet C. Improved criteria and comparative genomics tool provide new insights into grass paleogenomics. Brief Bioinform. 2009;10:619–30." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR100" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2817">100</a>]. The coordinate of conserved genes among the three genomes were extracted from these alignments that belong to syntenic blocks identified by McscanX [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 95" title="Wang YP, Tang HB, Debarry JD, Tan X, Li JP, Wang XY, et al. MCScanX: a toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and collinearity. Nucleic Acids Res. 2012;40:e49." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR95" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2820">95</a>]. Then the syntenic blocks were merged using GRIMM [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 101" title="Bourque G, Pevzner PA. Genomescale evolution: reconstructing gene orders in the ancestral species. Genome Res. 2002;12:26–36." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR101" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2823">101</a>] to get the coordinate correspondence between the three collinear groups. Finally, the MGR [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 102" title="Lin CH, Zhao H, Lowcay SH, Shahab A, Bourque G. webMGR: an online tool for the multiple genome rearrangement problem.
