Bioinformatics. 2009;26:408–10." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR102" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2826">102</a>] software was used to rearrange the multiple genomes to get the ancestral gene orders.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">Comparative analysis of LTR retrotransposons</h3><p>Intact long terminal repeat retrotransposons (LTR) were identified by searching the genomes of <i>C. praecox</i> with LTRharvest [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 103" title="Ellinghaus D, Kurtz S, Willhoeft U. LTRharvest, an efficient and flexible software for de novo detection of LTR retrotransposons. BMC Bioinformatics. 2008;9:18." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR103" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2840">103</a>] (-motif tgca -motifmis 1) and LTR_Finder [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Xu Z, Wang H. LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 2007;35:W265–8." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2843">69</a>] (LTR length 100 to 5000 nt; length between two LTRs: 1000 to 20,000 nt). A two-step procedure was used to filter the candidate sequences to reduce false positives. First, the primer binding site (PBS) motif was identified by LTR digest [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 104" title="Steinbiss S, Willhoeft U, Gremme G, Kurtz S. Fine-grained annotation and classification of de novo predicted LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 2009;37:7002–13." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR104" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2846">104</a>] based on the predicted tRNA sequences from tRNAscan-SE [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Nawrocki EP, Kolbe DL, Eddy SR. Infernal 1.0: inference of RNA alignments. Bioinformatics. 2009;25:1335–7." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2849">72</a>], and only elements that contained PBS were retained; next, protein domains (pol, gag, and env) in candidate LTR retrotransposons were identified by searching against HMM profiles collected by Gypsy Database (GyDB) (<a href="http://gydb.org/">http://gydb.org/</a>). Elements containing a gag domain, protease domain, reverse transcriptase (RT) domain, and integrase domain were considered as intact. Second, families of these intact LTR retrotransposons were clustered using the SiLiX software package (<a href="http://lbbe.univ-lyon1.fr/SiLiX">lbbe.univ-lyon1.fr/SiLiX</a>) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 105" title="Miele V, Penel S, Duret L. Ultra-fast sequence clustering from similarity networks with SiLiX. BMC Bioinformatics. 2011;12:116." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR105" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2867">105</a>]. Finally, LTRs that did not contain protein domains or that belonged to families with less than 2 members were discarded. Each nucleic acid diversity (<i>λ</i>) was calculated from the MUSLCE alignment of the 5′and 3′ LTR sequences of LTR-RTs by EMBOSS program distmat. Using a substitution rate (<i>r</i>) of 1.51 × 10<sup>− 9</sup> substitutions per site per year [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Chen JH, Hao ZD, Guang XM, Zhao CX, Wang PK, Xue LJ, et al. Liriodendron genome sheds light on angiosperm phylogeny and species–pair differentiation. Nat Plants. 2019;5:18." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d25660e2879">14</a>], the insertion date (<i>T</i>) was computed for each LTR-RT (<i>T</i> = <i>K</i>/2<i>r</i>, <i>K</i>: genetic distance, <i>K</i> = − 0.75ln(1 − 4<i>λ</i>/3)).</p><p>The sequences of intact LTR-RT identified in <i>C. praecox</i>, <i>C. chinensis</i>, <i>C. kanehirae</i>, and <i>L. chinense</i> genomes were translated according to 3-frames. The translated amino acid sequences were then searched for the <i>Ty1/Copia</i> (PF07727) and <i>Ty3/Gypsy</i> (PF000078) domains using HMMER (version 3.1b2, <a href="http://hmmer.org">http://hmmer.org</a>) (E-value ≤1e<sup>− 5</sup>). The target hit amino acid sequences of <i>Copia</i> and <i>Gypsy</i> superfamilies were aligned using mafft (<a href="https://mafft.cbrc.jp/alignment/software/">https://mafft.cbrc.jp/alignment/software/</a>) with default parameters. The phylogenetic trees of <i>Copia</i>-like and <i>Gypsy</i>-like LTR-RTs were constructed using fastTree (<a href="http://www.microbesonline.org/fasttree/">http://www.microbesonline.org/fasttree/</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec24">Histological examination</h3><p>To investigate the floral developmental characteristics of wintersweet, we performed a detailed developmental study from the initiation to the opening of the flower bud. Data collection was initiated on the day that the floral buds were visible to the naked eye. The sampling of <i>C. praecox</i> began on April 11, 2016, and ended on February 6, 2017. Ten axillary buds on current-year-old short branches were collected at random every 7 days from three trees. Five homogenized buds were used for histological examination and the others were frozen in liquid nitrogen and stored at − 80 °C for further RNA sequencing.</p><p>Flower buds at various stages of development were fixed in FAA (70% ethanol: formalin: acetic acid = 5:5:90) for more than 24 h. Then, 8-μm-thick microtome sections were made after embedding in paraffin wax. These sections were mounted on slides and stained with 1% safranine and 0.5% fast green. Slides were visually examined and photographed using a Nikon Eclipse 80i digital imaging microscope equipped with an electronic photo system. Based on the observation and the annual morphological changes of flower buds, we divided the flower developmental process into nine stages (FBS1–9) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#Fig4">4</a>a).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec25">RNA isolation, library construction, and sequencing</h3><p>The flower buds or whole flowers with three biological replicates were harvested at 14 stages including FBS1–9 and S1–5 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#Fig4">4</a> and Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#MOESM2">2</a>: Fig. S18) respectively. Total RNA from flower buds or whole flowers at different stages was extracted using an RNA prep pure Plant RNA Purification Kit (Tiangen Biotech, Beijing, China). RNA quality was verified using an Agilent 2100 (Agilent Technologies, CA, USA) plus electrophoresis system. A total of 42 cDNA libraries were prepared and sequenced on an Illumina HiSeq 2500 platform using paired-end cycles.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec26">Analysis of genes involved in the formation of floral scent</h3><p>Using terpene and phenylpropanoid biosynthesis pathway genes in <i>A. thaliana</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Vranová E, Coman D, Gruissem W. Network analysis of the MVA and MEP pathways for isoprenoid synthesis. Annu Rev Plant Biol. 2013;64:665–700." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d25660e3005">43</a>] and <i>Petunia hybrida</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Maeda H, Dudareva N. The shikimate pathway and aromatic amino acid biosynthesis in plants. Annu Rev Plant Biol. 2012;63:73–105." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d25660e3011">45</a>] as bait, the corresponding genes in <i>C. praecox</i> were identified based on genome annotation and local blast search against wintersweet genome with a filtered parameter (<i>E</i>-value &lt; 10<sup>− 5</sup>, identity ≥50%, and coverage ≥30%). For larger-size gene families, the genes were determined by combining local blast reports and phylogenetic analysis to distinguish the orthologs of corresponding functionally characterized genes.</p><p>To identify the <i>CpTPS</i> genes in <i>C. praecox</i> and <i>C. chinensis</i>, two Pfam domains: PF01397 and PF03936 were used to search against the proteome using HMMER [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 106" title="Wheeler TJ, Eddy SR. nhmmer: DNA homology search with profile HMMs. Bioinformatics. 2013;29:2487–9." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR106" id="ref-link-section-d25660e3035">106</a>]. The target hits (<i>E</i>-value &lt; 10<sup>− 5</sup>) were identified as candidate terpene synthase genes. Synteny and collinearity between these genes were analyzed using MCScanX [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 95" title="Wang YP, Tang HB, Debarry JD, Tan X, Li JP, Wang XY, et al. MCScanX: a toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and collinearity. Nucleic Acids Res. 2012;40:e49." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR95" id="ref-link-section-d25660e3044">95</a>]. Based on the chromosome location in the gff files, the structure and chromosomal distribution of <i>CpTPS</i> genes were illustrated with TBtools [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 107" title="Chen CJ, Xia R, Chen H, He YH. TBtools, a toolkit for biologists integrating various biological data handling tools with a user-friendly interface. BioRxiv. 2018;289660. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1016/j.molp.2020.06.009&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR107" id="ref-link-section-d25660e3050">107</a>]. The 2000-bp region upstream of <i>CpTPS</i> genes was defined as the promoter region, in which the cis-acting regulatory elements were identified with PlantCARE [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 108" title="Lescot M, Déhais P, Thijs G, Marchal K, Moreau Y, Peer YVD, Rouzéet P, Rombauts S. PlantCARE, a database of plant cis-acting regulatory elements and a portal to tools for in silico analysis of promoter sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 2002;30:325–7." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR108" id="ref-link-section-d25660e3056">108</a>] and PLACE [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 109" title="Higo K, Ugawa Y, Iwamoto M, Korenagaet T. Plant cis-acting regulatory DNA elements (PLACE) database: 1999. Nucleic Acids Res. 1999;27:297–300." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR109" id="ref-link-section-d25660e3060">109</a>] databases. A heat map was generated with the TBtools [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 107" title="Chen CJ, Xia R, Chen H, He YH. TBtools, a toolkit for biologists integrating various biological data handling tools with a user-friendly interface. BioRxiv. 2018;289660. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1016/j.molp.2020.06.009&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR107" id="ref-link-section-d25660e3063">107</a>] based on the expression level of wintersweet <i>TPS</i> genes at different floral developmental stages. <i>A. thaliana</i> and <i>O. sativa TPSs</i> were downloaded from phytozome (<a href="https://phytozome.jgi.doe.gov/pz/portal.html">https://phytozome.jgi.doe.gov/pz/portal.html</a>). All the phylogenetic trees of functional genes were constructed by maximum likelihood method with MEGAX [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 110" title="Kumar S, Stecher G, Li M, Knyaz C, Tamura K. MEGA X: molecular evolutionary genetics analysis across computing platforms. Mol Biol Evol. 2018;35:1547–9." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR110" id="ref-link-section-d25660e3083">110</a>] using the amino acid sequence alignments generated by ClustalX [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 111" title="Larkin MA, Blackshields G, Brown NP, Chenna R, McGettiganet PA, McWilliam H, et al. Clustal W and Clustal X version 2.0. Bioinformatics. 2007;23:2947–8." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR111" id="ref-link-section-d25660e3086">111</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec27">Characterization of the <i>TPS</i> genes</h3><p>The ORF of the three <i>TPS</i> genes (<i>CpTPS4</i>, <i>CpTPS9</i>, and <i>CpTPS42</i>) were isolated and subcloned into the BamHI/XhoI sites of pET28a using ClonExpress II One Step Cloning Kit (<a href="http://www.vazymebiotech.com/index.html">http://www.vazymebiotech.com/index.html</a>) according to the manufacturer’s introductions. The recombinant plasmids and pET28a lacking an insert (control) were transformed into <i>E. coli</i> BL21 (DE3) competent cells. After 0.5 mM IPTG induction at 16 °C overnight, the proteins were harvested and purified by affinity chromatography on nickel-nitrilotriacetic acid-agarose (Qiagen, <a href="http://www.qiagen.com">http://www.qiagen.com</a>). The purity of the isolated proteins was verified by the densitometry of the SDS-PAGE gels after Coomassie Brilliant Blue staining. The concentration of purified proteins was determined by the Bradford method [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 112" title="Bradford MM. A rapid and sensitive method for the quantitation of microgram quantities of protein utilizing the principle of protein-dye binding. Anal Biochem. 1976;72:248–54." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#ref-CR112" id="ref-link-section-d25660e3131">112</a>].</p><p>Assays for TPS protein activity were carried out in a 1 ml assay buffer (30 mM HEPES, pH 7.5, 5 mM DTT, 25 mM MgCl<sub>2</sub>) containing 10 μg purified CpTPS proteins and 60μΜ GPP/FPP. The mixture was incubated at 30 °C for an hour and then 45 °C for 15 min. After incubation, the synthesized volatiles were collected using the DVB/CAR/PDMS headspace sampler and analyzed as Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-02088-y#MOESM3">3</a>: Supplementary Note 2.</p></div></div></section>

              <p>All raw sequencing reads have been deposited in the NCBI Sequence Read Archive (<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra">https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra</a>) under project PRJNA600650 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 113" title="Shang JZ, Tian JP, Cheng HH, Yan QM, Li L, Jamal A, Xu ZP, Xiang L, Saski C, Jin SX, Zhao KG, Liu XQ, Chen LQ.                 </p></li><li class="c-article-references__item js-c-reading-companion-references-item" itemprop="citation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/ScholarlyArticle"><meta itemprop="author" content="MS.
